# Investigation: Django ADMINS/MANAGERS Settings Format Migration Audit

**Repository:** django/django
**Task Type:** Migration Audit (investigation only — no code changes)

## Scenario

Django is changing the `ADMINS` and `MANAGERS` settings from their current format of `[(name, email), ...]` tuples to simple `[email, ...]` string lists. This is tracked in ticket #36138. Before executing this migration, the team needs a comprehensive audit of all code that reads, validates, or processes these settings.

## Your Task

Produce a migration audit report at `/logs/agent/investigation.md`.

Your report MUST cover:
1. Every file that reads `settings.ADMINS` or `settings.MANAGERS`
2. Every file that validates or type-checks the settings format
3. Every file that unpacks the tuple format (e.g., `for name, email in settings.ADMINS`)
4. All documentation references that show the old tuple format
5. Test files that use the old format in fixtures or assertions
6. Third-party compatibility concerns (what breaks for users with old-format settings)
7. A migration checklist: each file that needs changes, what change is needed

## Output Requirements

Write your investigation report to `/logs/agent/investigation.md` with these sections:

```
# Investigation Report

## Summary
<1-2 sentence finding>

## Root Cause
<Core code paths that consume the tuple format>

## Evidence
<Code references with file paths and line numbers>

## Affected Components
<List of modules/packages impacted>

## Recommendation
<Migration checklist with specific files and changes needed>
```

## Constraints

- Do NOT write any code
- Do NOT modify any source files
- Your job is investigation and analysis only
- Focus on `django/core/mail/`, test files, and documentation

    40→The model name used in this setting should be all lowercase, regardless of the
    41→case of the actual model class name.
    42→
    43→.. setting:: ADMINS
    44→
    45→``ADMINS``
    46→----------
    47→
    48→Default: ``[]`` (Empty list)
    49→
    50→A list of all the people who get code error notifications. When
    51→:setting:`DEBUG=False <DEBUG>` and :class:`~django.utils.log.AdminEmailHandler`
    52→is configured in :setting:`LOGGING` (done by default), Django emails these
    53→people the details of exceptions raised in the request/response cycle.
    54→
    55→Each item in the list should be a tuple of (Full name, email address). Example::
    56→
    57→    [("John", "john@example.com"), ("Mary", "mary@example.com")]
    58→
    59→.. setting:: ALLOWED_HOSTS
    60→
    61→``ALLOWED_HOSTS``
    62→-----------------
    63→
    64→Default: ``[]`` (Empty list)
    65→
    66→A list of strings representing the host/domain names that this Django site can
    67→serve. This is a security measure to prevent :ref:`HTTP Host header attacks
    68→<host-headers-virtual-hosting>`, which are possible even under many
    69→seemingly-safe web server configurations.
    70→
    71→Values in this list can be fully qualified names (e.g. ``'www.example.com'``),
    72→in which case they will be matched against the request's ``Host`` header
    73→exactly (case-insensitive, not including port). A value beginning with a period
    74→can be used as a subdomain wildcard: ``'.example.com'`` will match
    75→``example.com``, ``www.example.com``, and any other subdomain of
    76→``example.com``. A value of ``'*'`` will match anything; in this case you are
    77→responsible to provide your own validation of the ``Host`` header (perhaps in a
    78→middleware; if

     1→=============================
     2→How to manage error reporting
     3→=============================
     4→
     5→When you're running a public site you should always turn off the
     6→:setting:`DEBUG` setting. That will make your server run much faster, and will
     7→also prevent malicious users from seeing details of your application that can be
     8→revealed by the error pages.
     9→
    10→However, running with :setting:`DEBUG` set to ``False`` means you'll never see
    11→errors generated by your site -- everyone will instead see your public error
    12→pages. You need to keep track of errors that occur in deployed sites, so Django
    13→can be configured to create reports with details about those errors.
    14→
    15→Email reports
    16→=============
    17→
    18→Server errors
    19→-------------
    20→
    21→When :setting:`DEBUG` is ``False``, Django will email the users listed in the
    22→:setting:`ADMINS` setting whenever your code raises an unhandled exception and
    23→results in an internal server error (strictly speaking, for any response with
    24→an HTTP status code of 500 or greater). This gives the administrators immediate
    25→notification of any errors. The :setting:`ADMINS` will get a description of the
    26→error, a complete Python traceback, and details about the HTTP request that
    27→caused the error.
    28→
    29→.. note::
    30→
    31→   In order to send email, Django requires a few settings telling it
    32→   how to connect to your mail server. At the very least, you'll need
    33→   to specify :setting:`EMAIL_HOST` and possibly
    34→   :setting:`EMAIL_HOST_USER` and :setting:`EMAIL_HOST_PASSWORD`,
    35→   though other settings may be also required depending on your mail
    36→   server's co
... (truncated)

     1→import socket
     2→
     3→from django.core.mail import mail_admins, mail_managers, send_mail
     4→from django.core.management.base import BaseCommand
     5→from django.utils import timezone
     6→
     7→
     8→class Command(BaseCommand):
     9→    help = "Sends a test email to the email addresses specified as arguments."
    10→    missing_args_message = (
    11→        "You must specify some email recipients, or pass the --managers or --admin "
    12→        "options."
    13→    )
    14→
    15→    def add_arguments(self, parser):
    16→        parser.add_argument(
    17→            "email",
    18→            nargs="*",
    19→            help="One or more email addresses to send a test email to.",
    20→        )
    21→        parser.add_argument(
    22→            "--managers",
    23→            action="store_true",
    24→            help="Send a test email to the addresses specified in settings.MANAGERS.",
    25→        )
    26→        parser.add_argument(
    27→            "--admins",
    28→            action="store_true",
    29→            help="Send a test email to the addresses specified in settings.ADMINS.",
    30→        )
    31→
    32→    def handle(self, *args, **kwargs):
    33→        subject = "Test email from %s on %s" % (socket.gethostname(), timezone.now())
    34→
    35→        send_mail(
    36→            subject=subject,

143-usertool_resultRead
   165→=================
   166→
   167→.. function:: mail_admins(subject, message, fail_silently=False, connection=None, html_message=None)
   168→
   169→``django.core.mail.mail_admins()`` is a shortcut for sending an email to the
   170→site admins, as defined in the :setting:`ADMINS` setting.
   171→
   172→``mail_admins()`` prefixes the subject with the value of the
   173→:setting:`EMAIL_SUBJECT_PREFIX` setting, which is ``"[Django] "`` by default.
   174→
   175→The "From:" header of the email will be the value of the
   176→:setting:`SERVER_EMAIL` setting.
   177→
   178→This method exists for convenience and readability.
   179→
   180→If ``html_message`` is provided, the resulting email will be a
   181→:mimetype:`multipart/alternative` email with ``message`` as the
   182→:mimetype:`text/plain` content type and ``html_message`` as the
   183→:mimetype:`text/html` content type.
   184→
   185→``mail_managers()``
   186→===================
   187→
   188→.. function:: mail_managers(subject, message, fail_silently=False, connection=None, html_message=None)
   189→
   190→``django.core.mail.mail_managers()`` is just like ``mail_admins()``, except it
   191→sends an email to the site managers, as defined in the :setting:`MANAGERS`
   192→setting.
   193→
   194→Examples
   195→========
   196→
   197→This sends a single email to john@example.com and jane@example.com, with them
   198→both appearing in the "To:"::
   199→
   200→    send_mail(
   201→        "Subject",
   202→        "Message.",
   203→        "from@example.com",
   204→        ["john@example.com", "jane@example.com"],

   315→
   316→.. class:: AdminEmailHandler(include_html=False, email_backend=None, reporter_class=None)
   317→
   318→    This handler sends an email to the site :setting:`ADMINS` for each log
   319→    message it receives.
   320→
   321→    If the log record contains a ``request`` attribute, the full details
   322→    of the request will be included in the email. The email subject will
   323→    include the phrase "internal IP" if the client's IP address is in the
   324→    :setting:`INTERNAL_IPS` setting; if not, it will include "EXTERNAL IP".
   325→
   326→    If the log record contains stack trace information, that stack
   327→    trace will be included in the email.
   328→
   329→    The ``include_html`` argument of ``AdminEmailHandler`` is used to
   330→    control whether the traceback email includes an HTML attachment
   331→    containing the full content of the debug web page that would have been
   332→    produced if :setting:`DEBUG` were ``True``. To set this value in your
   333→    configuration, include it in the handler definition for
   334→    ``django.utils.log.AdminEmailHandler``, like this::
   335→
   336→        "handlers": {
   337→            "mail_admins": {
   338→                "level": "ERROR",
   339→                "class": "django.utils.log.AdminEmailHandler",

   251→:setting:`ADMINS` and :setting:`MANAGERS`
   252→-----------------------------------------
   253→
   254→:setting:`ADMINS` will be notified of 500 errors by email.
   255→
   256→:setting:`MANAGERS` will be notified of 404 errors.
   257→:setting:`IGNORABLE_404_URLS` can help filter out spurious reports.
   258→
   259→See :doc:`/howto/error-reporting` for details on error reporting by email.
   260→
   261→.. admonition:: Error reporting by email doesn't scale very well
   262→
   263→    Consider using an error monitoring system such as Sentry_ before your
   264→    inbox is flooded by reports. Sentry can also aggregate logs.
   265→
   266→    .. _Sentry: https://docs.sentry.io/
   267→

   598→* Next, notice how the settings are annotated:
   599→
   600→  .. code-block:: rst
   601→
   602→    .. setting:: ADMINS
   603→
   604→    ADMINS
   605→    ======
   606→
   607→    Default: ``[]`` (Empty list)
   608→
   609→    A list of all the people who get code error notifications. When
   610→    ``DEBUG=False`` and a view raises an exception, Django will email these people
   611→    with the full exception information. Each member of the list should be a tuple
   612→    of (Full name, email address). Example::
   613→
   614→        [("John", "john@example.com"), ("Mary", "mary@example.com")]
   615→
   616→    Note that Django will email *all* of these people whenever an error happens.
   617→    See :doc:`/howto/error-reporting` for more information.
   618→
   619→  This marks up the following header as the "canonical" target for the
   620→  setting ``ADMINS``. This means any time I talk about ``ADMINS``,
   621→  I can reference it using ``:setting:`ADMINS```.
   622→
   623→That's basically how everything fits together.
   624→

   629→you'd like to help translate the documentation into another language.

   104→

   107→        raise ValueError("Invalid address; address parts cannot contain newlines.")

   110→    try:
   111→        nm.encode("ascii")
   112→        nm = Header(nm).encode()
   113→    except UnicodeEncodeError:
   114→        nm = Header(nm, encoding).encode()

    40→The model name used in this setting should be all lowercase, regardless of the

    43→.. setting:: ADMINS
    44→
    45→``ADMINS``
    46→----------
    47→
    48→Default: ``[]`` (Empty list)
    49→
    50→A list of all the people who get code error notifications. When
    51→:setting:`DEBUG=False <DEBUG>` and :class:`~django.utils.log.AdminEmailHandler`
    52→is configured in :setting:`LOGGING` (done by default), Django emails these
    53→people the details of exceptions raised in the request/response cycle.
    54→
    55→Each item in the list should be a tuple of (Full name, email address). Example::
    56→
    57→    [("John", "john@example.com"), ("Mary", "mary@example.com")]
    58→
    59→.. setting:: ALLOWED_HOSTS
    60→
    61→``ALLOWED_HOSTS``
    62→-----------------
    63→
    64→Default: ``[]`` (Empty list)
    65→
    66→A list of strings representing the host/domain names that this Django site can
    67→serve. This is a security measure to prevent :ref:`HTTP Host header attacks
    68→<host-headers-virtual-hosting>`, which are possible even under many
    69→seemingly-safe web server configurations.
    70→
    71→Values in this list can be fully qualified names (e.g. ``'www.example.com'``),
    72→in which case they will be matched against the request's ``Host`` header
    73→exactly (case-insensitive, not including port). A value beginning with a period
    74→can be used as a subdomain wildcard: ``'.example.com'`` will match
    75→``example.com``, ``www.example.com``, and any other subdomain of
    76→``example.com``. A value of ``'*'`` will match anything; in this case you are
    77→responsible to provide your own validation of the ``Host`` header (perhaps in a
    78→middleware; if
... (truncated)

    43→.. setting:: ADMINS
    44→
    45→``ADMINS``
    46→----------
    47→
    48→Default: ``[]`` (Empty list)
    49→
    50→A list of all the people who get code error notifications. When
    51→:setting:`DEBUG=False <DEBUG>` and :class:`~django.utils.log.AdminEmailHandler`
    52→is configured in :setting:`LOGGING` (done by default), Django emails these
    53→people the details of exceptions raised in the request/response cycle.

    56→

    58→

    61→``ALLOWED_HOSTS``
    62→-----------------
    63→
    64→Default: ``[]`` (Empty list)
    65→
    66→A list of strings representing the host/domain names that this Django site can
    67→serve. This is a security measure to prevent :ref:`HTTP Host header attacks
    68→<host-headers-virtual-hosting>`, which are possible even under many
    69→seemingly-safe web server configurations.

   611→    with the full exception information. Each member of the list should be a tuple
   612→    of (Full name, email address). Example::
   613→
   614→        [("John", "john@example.com"), ("Mary", "mary@example.com")]
   615→
   616→    Note that Django will email *all* of these people whenever an error happens.
   617→    See :doc:`/howto/error-reporting` for more information.
   618→
   619→  This marks up the following header as the "canonical" target for the
   620→  setting ``ADMINS``. This means any time I talk about ``ADMINS``,
   621→  I can reference it using ``:setting:`ADMINS```.
   622→
   623→That's basically how everything fits together.

The investigation report has been written to `/logs/agent/investigation.md`. Here is a summary of findings:

---

## Key Findings

**The entire tuple-format dependency flows through one function:** `_send_server_message()` in `django/core/mail/__init__.py:122–147`. It validates that every element of `ADMINS`/`MANAGERS` is a 2-tuple (line 135) and extracts the email at index `[1]` (line 142). Everything else calls `mail_admins()`/`mail_managers()` and is only indirectly affected.

**Files requiring code changes:**

| File | What must change |
|------|-----------------|
| `django/core/mail/__init__.py:135–136,142` | Replace 2-tuple validation with `isinstance(a, str)`; replace `a[1]` extraction with `a` directly |
| `django/conf/global_settings.py:24–25` | Update format comment |
| `docs/ref/settings.txt:55–57` | Update ADMINS example from tuple to plain string |
| `docs/internals/contributing/writing-documentation.txt:609–614` | Update RST tutorial example |
| `tests/mail/tests.py:1780–1884` | Rewrite tuple fixtures and update "list of 2-tuples" error message assertions |
| `tests/mail/test_sendtestemail.py:6–15` | Replace class-level fixture tuples with string lists |
| `tests/logging_tests/tests.py` (9 locations) | Replace `[("name", "email")]` fixtures with `["email"]` |
| `tests/middleware/tests.py:392` | Replace tuple fixture |
| `tests/view_tests/tests/test_debug.py:1454,1490,1533` | Replace tuple fixtures |

**Third-party concern:** Any project using the old `[(name, email)]` format will break. A deprecation path (accepting both formats with a warning for one LTS cycle) is strongly recommended before hard removal.

"     1\u2192=============================\n     2\u2192How to manage error reporting\n     3\u2192=============================\n     4\u2192\n     5\u2192When you're running a public site you should always turn off the\n     6\u2192:setting:`DEBUG` setting. That will make your server run much faster, and will\n     7\u2192also prevent malicious users from seeing details of your application that can be\n     8\u2192revealed by the error pages.\n     9\u2192\n    10\u2192However, running with :setting:`DEBUG` set to ``False`` means you'll never see\n    11\u2192errors generated by your site -- everyone will instead see your public error\n    12\u2192pages. You need to keep track of errors that occur in deployed sites, so Django\n    13\u2192can be configured to create reports with details about those errors.\n    14\u2192\n    15\u2192Email reports\n    16\u2192=============\n    17\u2192\n    18\u2192Server errors\n    19\u2192-------------\n    20\u2192\n    21\u2192When :setting:`DEBUG` is ``False``, Django will email the users listed in the\n    22\u2192:setting:`ADMINS` setting whenever your code raises an unhandled exception and\n    23\u2192results in an internal server error (strictly speaking, for any response with\n    24\u2192an HTTP status code of 500 or greater). This gives the administrators immediate\n    25\u2192notification of any errors. The :setting:`ADMINS` will get a description of the\n    26\u2192error, a complete Python traceback, and details about the HTTP request that\n    27\u2192caused the error.\n    28\u2192\n    29\u2192.. note::\n    30\u2192\n    31\u2192   In order to send email, Django requires a few settings telling it\n    32\u2192   how to connect to your mail server. At the very least, you'll need\n    33\u2192   to specify :setting:`EMAIL_HOST` and possibly\n    34\u2192   :setting:`EMAIL_HOST_USER` and :setting:`EMAIL_HOST_PASSWORD`,\n    35\u2192   though other settings may be also required depending on your mail\n    36\u2192   server's configuration. Consult :doc:`the Django settings\n    37\u2192   documentation </ref/settings>` for a full list of email-related\n    38\u2192   settings.\n    39\u2192\n    40\u2192By default, Django will send email from root@localhost. However, some mail\n    41\u2192providers reject all email from this address. To use a different sender\n    42\u2192address, modify the :setting:`SERVER_EMAIL` setting.\n    43\u2192\n    44\u2192To activate this behavior, put the email addresses of the recipients in the\n    45\u2192:setting:`ADMINS` setting.\n    46\u2192\n    47\u2192.. seealso::\n    48\u2192\n    49\u2192    Server error emails are sent using the logging framework, so you can\n    50\u2192    customize this behavior by :doc:`customizing your logging configuration\n    51\u2192    </topics/logging>`.\n    52\u2192\n    53\u2192404 errors\n    54\u2192----------\n    55\u2192\n    56\u2192Django can also be configured to email errors about broken links (404 \"page\n    57\u2192not found\" errors). Django sends emails about 404 errors when:\n    58\u2192\n    59\u2192* :setting:`DEBUG` is ``False``;\n    60\u2192\n    61\u2192* Your :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` setting includes\n    62\u2192  :class:`django.middleware.common.BrokenLinkEmailsMiddleware`.\n    63\u2192\n    64\u2192If those conditions are met, Django will email the users listed in the\n    65\u2192:setting:`MANAGERS` setting whenever your code raises a 404 and the request has\n    66\u2192a referer. It doesn't bother to email for 404s that don't have a referer --\n    67\u2192those are usually people typing in broken URLs or broken web bots. It also\n    68\u2192ignores 404s when the referer is equal to the requested URL, since this\n    69\u2192behavior is from broken web bots too.\n    70\u2192\n    71\u2192.. note::\n    72\u2192\n    73\u2192    :class:`~django.middleware.common.BrokenLinkEmailsMiddleware` must appear\n    74\u2192    before other middleware that intercepts 404 errors, such as\n    75\u2192    :class:`~django.middleware.locale.LocaleMiddleware` or\n    76\u2192    :class:`~django.contrib.flatpages.middleware.FlatpageFallbackMiddleware`.\n    77\u2192    Put it toward the top of your :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` setting.\n    78\u2192\n    79\u2192You can tell Django to stop reporting particular 404s by tweaking the\n    80\u2192:setting:`IGNORABLE_404_URLS` setting. It should be a list of compiled\n    81\u2192regular expression objects. For example::\n    82\u2192\n    83\u2192    import re\n    84\u2192\n    85\u2192    IGNORABLE_404_URLS = [\n    86\u2192        re.compile(r\"\\.(php|cgi)$\"),\n    87\u2192        re.compile(r\"^/phpmyadmin/\"),\n    88\u2192    ]\n    89\u2192\n    90\u2192In this example, a 404 to any URL ending with ``.php`` or ``.cgi`` will *not* be\n    91\u2192reported. Neither will any URL starting with ``/phpmyadmin/``.\n    92\u2192\n    93\u2192The following example shows how to exclude some conventional URLs that browsers and\n    94\u2192crawlers often request::\n    95\u2192\n    96\u2192    import re\n    97\u2192\n    98\u2192    IGNORABLE_404_URLS = [\n    99\u2192        re.compile(r\"^/apple-touch-icon.*\\.png$\"),\n   100\u2192        re.compile(r\"^/favicon\\.ico$\"),\n   101\u2192        re.compile(r\"^/robots\\.txt$\"),\n   102\u2192    ]\n   103\u2192\n   104\u2192(Note that these are regular expressions, so we put a backslash in front of\n   105\u2192periods to escape them.)\n   106\u2192\n   107\u2192If you'd like to customize the behavior of\n   108\u2192:class:`django.middleware.common.BrokenLinkEmailsMiddleware` further (for\n   109\u2192example to ignore requests coming from web crawlers), you should subclass it\n   110\u2192and override its methods.\n   111\u2192\n   112\u2192.. seealso::\n   113\u2192\n   114\u2192    404 errors are logged using the logging framework. By default, these log\n   115\u2192    records are ignored, but you can use them for error reporting by writing a\n   116\u2192    handler and :doc:`configuring logging </topics/logging>` appropriately.\n   117\u2192\n   118\u2192.. _filtering-error-reports:\n   119\u2192\n   120\u2192Filtering error reports\n   121\u2192=======================\n   122\u2192\n   123\u2192.. warning::\n   124\u2192\n   125\u2192    Filtering sensitive data is a hard problem, and it's nearly impossible to\n   126\u2192    guarantee that sensitive data won't leak into an error report. Therefore,\n   127\u2192    error reports should only be available to trusted team members and you\n   128\u2192    should avoid transmitting error reports unencrypted over the internet\n   129\u2192    (such as through email).\n   130\u2192\n   131\u2192Filtering sensitive information\n   132\u2192-------------------------------\n   133\u2192\n   134\u2192.. currentmodule:: django.views.decorators.debug\n   135\u2192\n   136\u2192Error reports are really helpful for debugging errors, so it is generally\n   137\u2192useful to record as much relevant information about those errors as possible.\n   138\u2192For example, by default Django records the `full traceback`_ for the\n   139\u2192exception raised, each `traceback frame`_\u2019s local variables, and the\n   140\u2192:class:`~django.http.HttpRequest`\u2019s :ref:`attributes<httprequest-attributes>`.\n   141\u2192\n   142\u2192However, sometimes certain types of information may be too sensitive and thus\n   143\u2192may not be appropriate to be kept track of, for example a user's password or\n   144\u2192credit card number. So in addition to filtering out settings that appear to be\n   145\u2192sensitive as described in the :setting:`DEBUG` documentation, Django offers a\n   146\u2192set of function decorators to help frame`: https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stack_frame\n   153\u2192\n   154\u2192.. function:: sensitive_variables(*variables)\n   155\u2192\n   156\u2192    If a function (either a view or any regular callback) in your code uses\n   157\u2192    local variables susceptible to contain sensitive information, you may\n   158\u2192    prevent the values of those variables from being included in error reports\n   159\u2192    using the ``sensitive_variables`` decorator::\n   160\u2192\n   161\u2192        from django.views.decorators.debug import sensitive_variables\n   162\u2192\n   163\u2192\n   164\u2192        @sensitive_variables(\"user\", \"pw\", \"cc\")\n   165\u2192        def process_info(user):\n   166\u2192            pw = user.pass_word\n   167\u2192            cc = user.credit_card_number\n   168\u2192            name = user.name\n   169\u2192            ...\n   170\u2192\n   171\u2192    In the above example, the values for the ``user``, ``pw`` and ``cc``\n   172\u2192    variables will be hidden and replaced with stars (``**********``)\n   173\u2192    in the error reports, whereas the value of the ``name`` variable will be\n   174\u2192    disclosed.\n   175\u2192\n   176\u2192    To systematically hide all local variables of a function from error logs,\n   177\u2192    do not provide any argument to the ``sensitive_variables`` decorator::\n   178\u2192\n   179\u2192        @sensitive_variables()\n   180\u2192        def my_function(): ...\n   181\u2192\n   182\u2192    .. admonition:: When using multiple decorators\n   183\u2192\n   184\u2192        If the variable you want to hide is also a function argument (e.g.\n   185\u2192        '``user``\u2019 in the following example), and if the decorated function has\n   186\u2192        multiple decorators, then make sure to place ``@sensitive_variables``\n   187\u2192        at the top of the decorator chain. This way it will also hide the\n   188\u2192        function argument as it gets passed through the other decorators::\n   189\u2192\n   190\u2192            @sensitive_variables(\"user\", \"pw\", \"cc\")\n   191\u2192            @some_decorator\n   192\u2192            @another_decorator\n   193\u2192            def process_info(user): ...\n   194\u2192\n   195\u2192.. function:: sensitive_post_parameters(*parameters)\n   196\u2192\n   197\u2192    If one of your views receives an :class:`~django.http.HttpRequest` object\n   198\u2192    with :attr:`POST parameters<django.http.HttpRequest.POST>` susceptible to\n   199\u2192    contain sensitive information, you may prevent the values of those\n   200\u2192    parameters from being included in the error reports using the\n   201\u2192    ``sensitive_post_parameters`` decorator::\n   202\u2192\n   203\u2192        from django.views.decorators.debug import sensitive_post_parameters\n reports\n   237\u2192--------------------\n   238\u2192\n   239\u2192All :func:`sensitive_variables` and :func:`sensitive_post_parameters` do is,\n   240\u2192respectively, annotate the decorated later be filtered out\n   243\u2192of reports when an error occurs. The actual filtering is done by Django's\n   244\u2192default error reporter filter:\n   245\u2192:class:`django.views.debug.SafeExceptionReporterFilter`. This filter uses the\n   246\u2192decorators' annotations to replace the corresponding values with stars\n   247\u2192(``**********``) when the error reports are produced. If you wish to\n   248\u2192override or customize this default behavior for your entire site, you need to\n   249\u2192define your own filter class and tell Django to use it via the\n   250\u2192:setting:`DEFAULT_EXCEPTION_REPORTER_FILTER` setting::\n   251\u2192\n   252\u2192    DEFAULT_EXCEPTION_REPORTER_FILTER = \"path.to.your.CustomExceptionReporterFilter\"\n   253\u2192\n   254\u2192You may also control in a more granular way which filter to use within any\n   255\u2192given view by setting the ``HttpRequest``\u2019s ``exception_reporter_filter``\n   256\u2192attribute::\n   257\u2192\n   258\u2192    def my_view(request):\n   259\u2192        if request.user.is_authenticated:\n   260\u2192            request.exception_reporter_filter = CustomExceptionReporterFilter()\n   261\u2192        ...\n   262\u2192\n   263\u2192.. currentmodule:: django.views.debug\n   264\u2192\n   265\u2192Your custom filter class needs to inherit from\n   266\u2192:class:`django.views.debug.SafeExceptionReporterFilter` and may override the\n   267\u2192following attributes and methods:\n   268\u2192\n   269\u2192.. class:: SafeExceptionReporterFilter\n   270\u2192\n   271\u2192    .. attribute:: cleansed_substitute\n   272\u2192\n   273\u2192        The string value to replace sensitive value with.   "content": "# Investigation Report\n\n## Summary\n\nDjango's `ADMINS` and `MANAGERS` settings are currently expected in a `[(name, email), ...]` tuple-of-2-tuples format. Migrating to a plain `[email, ...]` string list requires changes to: the validation logic and email-extraction logic in `django/core/mail/__init__.py`, the format comment in `django/conf/global_settings.py`, two documentation files that show the tuple example, and all test fixtures that supply the old format.\n\n## Root Cause\n\nThe core consumer of both settings is the private function `_send_server_message()` in `django/core/mail/__init__.py` (lines 122\u2013147). It:\n\n1. **Reads** the setting via `getattr(settings, setting_name)` (line 131).\n2. **Validates** that every element is a list or tuple with exactly 2 items (line 135):\n   ```python\n   if not all(isinstance(a, (list, tuple)) and len(a) == 2 for a in recipients):\n       raise ValueError(f\"The {setting_name} setting must be a list of 2-tuples.\")\n   ```\n3. **Extracts** the email address by taking index `[1]` from each tuple (line 142):\n   ```python\n   to=[a[1] for a in recipients],\n   ```\n\nAll higher-level callers (`mail_admins`, `mail_managers`, `AdminEmailHandler`, `BrokenLinkEmailsMiddleware`, `sendtestemail`) reach `_send_server_message` transitively and therefore inherit the tuple dependency.\n\n## Evidence\n\n### Source Files\n\n| File | Line(s) | Role | Detail |\n|------|---------|------|--------|\n| `django/conf/global_settings.py` | 24\u201326 | Defines ADMINS | Comment documents the tuple format: `[('Full Name', 'email@example.com'), ...]` |\n| `django/conf/global_settings.py` | 172\u2013174 | Defines MANAGERS | `MANAGERS = ADMINS` (inherits ADMINS default/comment) |\n| `django/core/mail/__init__.py` | 131 | Reads setting | `recipients = getattr(settings, setting_name)` |\n| `django/core/mail/__init__.py` | 135\u2013136 | **Validates** format | `isinstance(a, (list, tuple)) and len(a) == 2` \u2192 raises `ValueError` if not 2-tuple |\n| `django/core/mail/__init__.py` | 142 | **Extracts** email | `to=[a[1] for a in recipients]` \u2014 index 1 of each tuple |\n| `django/core/mail/__init__.py` | 150\u2013161 | `mail_admins()` | Passes `setting_name=\"ADMINS\"` to `_send_server_message` |\n| `django/core/mail/__init__.py` | 164\u2013175 | `mail_managers()` | Passes `setting_name=\"MANAGERS\"` to `_send_server_message` |\n| `django/utils/log.py` | 96\u2013101 | Checks ADMINS | `not settings.ADMINS` early-return guard in `AdminEmailHandler.emit()` |\n| `django/utils/log.py` | 137\u2013140 | Uses ADMINS | `AdminEmailHandler.send_mail()` calls `mail.mail_admins()` |\n| `django/middleware/common.py` | 118\u2013143 | Uses MANAGERS | `BrokenLinkEmailsMiddleware.process_response()` calls `mail_managers()` |\n| `django/core/management/commands/sendtestemail.py` | 42\u201346 | Uses both | Calls `mail_admins()` and `mail_managers()` based on CLI flags |\n\n### Documentation Files\n\n| File | Line(s) | Content |\n|------|---------|---------|\n| `docs/ref/settings.txt` | 55\u201357 | \"Each item in the list should be a tuple of (Full name, email address). Example:: `[(\"John\", \"john@example.com\"), (\"Mary\", \"mary@example.com\")]`\" |\n| `docs/ref/settings.txt` | 2066\u20132075 | MANAGERS described as \"A list in the same format as ADMINS\" \u2014 inherits tuple description |\n| `docs/internals/contributing/writing-documentation.txt` | 609\u2013614 | Uses ADMINS as a RST markup example, showing the same tuple format: `[(\"John\", \"john@example.com\"), (\"Mary\", \"mary@example.com\")]` |\n| `docs/howto/error-reporting.txt` | 21\u201327, 44\u201345, 65\u201368 | References ADMINS/MANAGERS for error emails and 404 notifications (no explicit format shown, but defers to settings.txt) |\n| `docs/howto/deployment/checklist.txt` | 251\u2013259 | Mentions ADMINS/MANAGERS for error notifications (no explicit format) |\n| `docs/topics/email.txt` | 167\u2013192 | Documents `mail_admins()` and `mail_managers()` functions (no explicit format) |\n| `docs/topics/logging.txt` | 393\u2013395 | References ADMINS in default logging config (no explicit format) |\n| `docs/ref/logging.txt` | 316\u2013319 | References ADMINS in `AdminEmailHandler` docs (no explicit format) |\n| `docs/ref/django-admin.txt` | 1051\u20131059 | `sendtestemail --managers`/`--admins` options (no explicit format) |\n| `docs/ref/middleware.txt` | 84\u201387 | `BrokenLinkEmailsMiddleware` references MANAGERS (no explicit format) |\n\n### Test Files\n\n| File | Line(s) | Format Used | Purpose |\n|------|---------|-------------|---------|\n| `tests/mail/test_sendtestemail.py` | 7\u201314 | **Old tuple** (class-level `@override_settings`) | Fixture for `sendtestemail` command tests; asserts specific email recipients by name |\n| `tests/mail/tests.py` | 1782\u20131803 | Old tuple (positional unpacking `for _, address in value`) | `test_mail_admins_and_managers` \u2014 explicitly tests that 2-tuple format is accepted |\n| `tests/mail/tests.py` | 1862\u20131884 | Old tuple (negative tests) | `test_wrong_admins_managers` \u2014 validates that non-2-tuple values raise `ValueError`; error message hardcodes \"list of 2-tuples\" |\n| `tests/mail/tests.py` | 1142, 1155 | Old tuple `[(\"nobody\", \"nobody@example.com\")]` | Connection argument tests for `mail_admins`/`mail_managers` |\n| `tests/mail/tests.py` | 1805, 1820, 1836\u20131837 | Old tuple `[(\"nobody\", \"nobody@example.com\")]` | HTML mail tests |\n| `tests/logging_tests/tests.py` | 251, 283, 322, 344, 375, 396, 438, 452, 570 | Old tuple `[(\"whatever admin\", \"admin@example.com\")]` | `AdminEmailHandler` tests |\n| `tests/middleware/tests.py` | 392 | Old tuple `[(\"PHD\", \"PHB@dilbert.com\")]` | `BrokenLinkEmailsMiddleware` test fixture |\n| `tests/view_tests/tests/test_debug.py` | 1454, 1490, 1533 | Old tuple `[(\"Admin\", \"admin@fattie-breakie.com\")]` | Debug email report tests |\n\n### Key Code Excerpt \u2014 Validation and Extraction\n\n```python\n# django/core/mail/__init__.py, lines 135\u2013142\nif not all(isinstance(a, (list, tuple)) and len(a) == 2 for a in recipients):\n    raise ValueError(f\"The {setting_name} setting must be a list of 2-tuples.\")\n\nmail = EmailMultiAlternatives(\n    ...\n    to=[a[1] for a in recipients],   # extracts index-1 (email) from each 2-tuple\n    ...\n)\n```\n\n## Affected Components\n\n1. **`django/core/mail/`** \u2014 Primary consumer; `_send_server_message`, `mail_admins`, `mail_managers` all depend on tuple format.\n2. **`django/conf/global_settings.py`** \u2014 Default definition comment documents old format.\n3. **`django/utils/log.py`** \u2014 `AdminEmailHandler` transitively depends via `mail_admins()`.\n4. **`django/middleware/common.py`** \u2014 `BrokenLinkEmailsMiddleware` transitively depends via `mail_managers()`.\n5. **`django/core/management/commands/sendtestemail.py`** \u2014 Management command (no direct format dependency, but calls mail_admins/mail_managers).\n6. **`docs/ref/settings.txt`** \u2014 Canonical format specification shows tuple examples.\n7. **`docs/internals/contributing/writing-documentation.txt`** \u2014 Duplicate tuple example used as RST markup tutorial.\n8. **`tests/mail/tests.py`** \u2014 Tests exercise the tuple format and assert the \"list of 2-tuples\" error message.\n9. **`tests/mail/test_sendtestemail.py`** \u2014 Class fixture uses old tuple format.\n10. **`tests/logging_tests/tests.py`** \u2014 Multiple test methods use old tuple format.\n11. **`tests/middleware/tests.py`** \u2014 `BrokenLinkEmailsMiddleware` test uses old tuple format.\n12. **`tests/view_tests/tests/test_debug.py`** \u2014 Debug email tests use old tuple format.\n\n## Recommendation\n\n### Migration Checklist\n\n#### 1. `django/core/mail/__init__.py` \u2014 **Critical**\n- **Lines 135\u2013136**: Replace the `isinstance(a, (list, tuple)) and len(a) == 2` validation with a check that each element `a` is a plain string (e.g., `isinstance(a, str)`).\n- **Line 136**: Update the error message from `\"must be a list of 2-tuples\"` to reflect the new expected format.\n- **Line 142**: Replace `to=[a[1] for a in recipients]` with `to=list(recipients)` (or equivalent), since each element will now be the email address directly.\n\n#### 2. `django/conf/global_settings.py` \u2014 **Low risk**\n- **Lines 24\u201325**: Update the comment from `# [('Full Name', 'email@example.com'), ...]` to `# ['email@example.com', ...]` (or remove the name component).\n\n#### 3. `docs/ref/settings.txt` \u2014 **Documentation**\n- **Lines 55\u201357**: Replace the format description (\"a tuple of (Full name, email address)\") and the example (`[(\"John\", \"john@example.com\"), ...]`) with the new string-list format.\n- **Lines 2066\u20132075**: MANAGERS description defers to ADMINS, so the ADMINS update covers it; verify the wording still reads correctly.\n\n#### 4. `docs/internals/contributing/writing-documentation.txt` \u2014 **Documentation**\n- **Lines 609\u2013614**: Update the RST example for ADMINS (used as a documentation tutorial) to show the new format.\n\n#### 5. `tests/mail/tests.py` \u2014 **Tests**\n- **Lines 1780\u20131803** (`test_mail_admins_and_managers`): Replace all tuple test values with plain email strings; update `for _, address in value` unpacking to `for address in value`.\n- **Lines 1862\u20131884** (`test_wrong_admins_managers`): Rewrite to test *new* invalid inputs (e.g., a list of tuples, non-string elements). Update the hardcoded error message string (`\"list of 2-tuples\"`) to match whatever the new error message says.\n- **Lines 1140\u20131165**: Replace `[(\"nobody\", \"nobody@example.com\")]` fixtures with `[\"nobody@example.com\"]`.\n- **Lines 1805, 1820, 1836\u20131837**: Replace `[(\"nobody\", ...)]` fixtures with plain string lists.\n\n#### 6. `tests/mail/test_sendtestemail.py` \u2014 **Tests**\n- **Lines 6\u201315**: Replace the class-level `@override_settings` fixture from tuple format to string list. Update recipient assertions (lines 70\u201375, 86\u201391, 102\u2013115) which currently assert on specific email addresses extracted from the tuple \u2014 the addresses themselves stay the same, so recipient assertions should still pass once the setting format is updated.\n\n#### 7. `tests/logging_tests/tests.py` \u2014 **Tests**\n- **Lines 251, 283, 322, 344, 375, 396, 438, 452, 570**: Replace all `[(\"admin name\", \"admin@example.com\")]` fixtures with `[\"admin@example.com\"]`. The `.to` assertions check for plain email strings, so those should remain valid.\n\n#### 8. `tests/middleware/tests.py` \u2014 **Tests**\n- **Line 392**: Replace `[(\"PHD\", \"PHB@dilbert.com\")]` with `[\"PHB@dilbert.com\"]`.\n\n#### 9. `tests/view_tests/tests/test_debug.py` \u2014 **Tests**\n- **Lines 1454, 1490, 1533**: Replace `[(\"Admin\", \"admin@fattie-breakie.com\")]` with `[\"admin@fattie-breakie.com\"]`.\n\n### Third-Party Compatibility Concerns\n\nAny third-party Django project or package that has ever set `ADMINS` or `MANAGERS` in the old `[(name, email), ...]` format will break silently or with an error after this migration:\n\n- **Silent behavior change**: If the new code accepts plain strings, a project still using the old tuple format will either raise an error (if validation still rejects non-strings) or cause malformed email headers (if strings like `\"('Admin', 'admin@example.com')\"` are used as addresses).\n- **Custom `AdminEmailHandler` subclasses**: Any handler that overrides `send_mail()` and accesses `settings.ADMINS` directly will need updating.\n- **Third-party middleware** using `settings.MANAGERS` directly (bypassing `mail_managers()`) will need updating.\n- **A deprecation path** should be considered: accept both formats during a transition period, warning on the old format, and removing support in a future major release. The Django deprecation policy typically requires at least one LTS cycle of deprecation warnings before removal.\n",

1. WRITE /logs/agent/investigation.md
# Investigation Report

## Summary

Django's `ADMINS` and `MANAGERS` settings are currently expected in a `[(name, email), ...]` tuple-of-2-tuples format. Migrating to a plain `[email, ...]` string list requires changes to: the validation logic and email-extraction logic in `django/core/mail/__init__.py`, the format comment in `django/conf/global_settings.py`, two documentation files that show the tuple example, and all test fixtures that supply the old format.

## Root Cause

The core consumer of both settings is the private function `_send_server_message()` in `django/core/mail/__init__.py` (lines 122–147). It:

1. **Reads** the setting via `getattr(settings, setting_name)` (line 131).
2. **Validates** that every element is a list or tuple with exactly 2 items (line 135):
   ```python
   if not all(isinstance(a, (list, tuple)) and len(a) == 2 for a in recipients):
       raise ValueError(f"The {setting_name} setting must be a list of 2-tuples.")
   ```
3. **Extracts** the email address by taking index `[1]` from each tuple (line 142):
   ```python
   to=[a[1] for a in recipients],
   ```

All higher-level callers (`mail_admins`, `mail_managers`, `AdminEmailHandler`, `BrokenLinkEmailsMiddleware`, `sendtestemail`) reach `_send_server_message` transitively and therefore inherit the tuple dependency.

## Evidence

### Source Files

| File | Line(s) | Role | Detail |
|------|---------|------|--------|
| `django/conf/global_settings.py` | 24–26 | Defines ADMINS | Comment documents the tuple format: `[('Full Name', 'email@example.com'), ...]` |
| `django/conf/global_settings.py` | 172–174 | Defines MANAGERS | `MANAGERS = ADMINS` (inherits ADMINS default/comment) |
| `django/core/mail/__init__.py` | 131 | Reads setting | `recipients = getattr(settings, setting_name)` |
| `dja
... (truncated)
